And if you think that the figure is already altered in some manner, then take it and separate it from the third day; and then rectify what has been altered of it and fill it with powder of moly, or album of egg, or flour of cypress, since you will have applied the cerate of it, and place on the plaster a woolen cloth.
And then when the plaster is attached and adjusted on it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, so that it is not removed until it is healed and the fracture is strengthened; for many times observed this fracture is strengthened in three weeks.
And if another observed aposteme occurs, then administer what we have often said in the treatment of it until it is healed, and the aposteme is reduced.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if it is a piece of bone separated or fragmented, then you subtly remove those fragments with what is convenient for the remedy of those disturbances.
And if the edges of the wound are tight, then you enlarge it with a flea-cut according to the quantity of your need.
And then return the injured part to its original state, and if it is not fully restored, place a suitable plaster on that area and attach it so that it adheres until it heals.
Chapter five.
On the restoration of the hernia when it is strained.
When the hernia is ruptured in the division before the navel, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
When it is ruptured, or separated, or separated into two parts that do not touch the surrounding tissues; and it is easier to restore; or it is such that it touches the surrounding tissues, and it is difficult to restore; or it is such that the fracture is in the wound.
And the operation on it, when the fracture is in the wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the adjunct and follows the ruptured hernia, and the other stretches out the skin with that division.
Then shape the fracture with your fingers until its form is made according to what is suitable, and it should not be in a prominent or hollow place.
And if you need more extension, then it is suitable that you place a piece of cloth or linen under the patient's thigh, and its size should be according to the quantity of your need; and stretch and elevate the hernia and tie the cloth with your hand until it shapes the fracture as is suitable.
And if you cannot reach the extremity of the furcula to the divided parts in front, because it has come to the pubic area, then it is necessary that the patient be placed on their back, and a cushion be put on the muscle of their thigh, in its middle part.
And the minister presses the muscle of their thigh at the divided parts until the bone of the furcula is elevated that is in the pubic area in its place, and then you correct the fracture and bind it with ties to the thighs.
More often if you feel stagnations of the furcula and they are swollen, then it is necessary that you make an incision over that area and extract those stagnations with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you make an incision on it with one of the incision tools that you have prepared for this purpose, since you had previously placed the furcula under the instrument that serves the ciphac; and the instrument is made of wood or iron, of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to what you need from it, according to the size of the bone or the smallness of it.
Measure the length of it according to what is possible for the operation; that it should have two extremities thus at times, and one extremity should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound that you have made when you extracted the abscess from the bone is wide, and there is a risk of gathering pus, then adjust the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear gathering pus, then fill the wound with dressings and poultices according to the quantity of the split of the wound.
And if there is a gathering of pus, then put on a dressing made with sour wine and rose oil, and apply it to that area.
And you must ensure that the steel is not rubbed against the broken bone; and for this reason, as you visit the patient every day, when the ligament is softened or the steel has been reapplied, then you correct this and strengthen the binding.
And then you stop the patient's sleep by their neck, and place a small cushion under their arm in the night, so that when they are elevated by the assistant on that side, thus the fracture of the shoulder is elevated to the elevation of the muscle; or their arm is tied to the neck.
And do not untie the binding unless there is an abscess or apostema in the area, within twelve days.
And then you renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it to that effect, and adjust in the constriction and restore it, and let it be until it is restored and the fracture of the shoulder is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in 28 days, and some visits are made at home in less than a month's time of that.
Chapter six.
On the restoration of the fracture of the shoulder.
The flat spatula is broken in the left side and it is not the breakage of it, but rather its edges.
And thus when the place of it is broken  and it is broken in the middle of its place  and not joined except by touch, then according to the shape of the fracture prepare it and reduce it according to its natural shape in every manner of art that is at your disposal.
And then apply a poultice of ground album of egg to the place, and stop it with a linen cloth; and apply dust of double-folded cloth; and apply on it a subtle plaster made of table or mortar according to the quantity of the spatula, all or a little more.
But if the plaster is concave in some of the places of the spatula, then you should fill that concavity with a linen cloth, and apply the plaster equally.
And then strengthen it with a long bandage from constricted binding and from firmness of the plaster, so that it does not move from its place.
And check the dressing every day; and all visits that it should be softened, remove another visit and strengthen it and adjust the plaster if it has moved from its place.
And let the patient lie on their side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And then when the name is completed, you dissolve the ligament, and you should be assured that the dissolution is not of the bones, but rather of the cartilage, which is not a serious or worrisome condition.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone to the skin and put a plaster on it, then cut above it, and remove the splinter, and do as we have said in the treatment of the fracture with a hot poultice of emollient, or apply a cautery if it becomes necessary here.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot, having been broken in the middle part of it, and this causes a deformation; but the extremities of the movement are not deformed, rather they are shaken and dislocated.
And of the accidents of it, when the middle of it is broken, it is that it declines to the inner parts and causes great pain, and difficulty in walking, and coughing, and vomiting blood; and it is declared in the cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden from feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient should be placed on their back, and a cushion under their shoulders; and apply pressure to the muscles of it, and adjust the ribs of it with the hands of two assistants, and proceed subtly in the operation and in the adjustment according to all manner that is possible and convenient for it, until the shape of the bone returns as it should be.
And then pause on the plaster and stop and pause above the subtle table of sauce or of warm poultice and of its similar things in lightness, since you envelop that in a cloth; and then subtly apply it to the broken bone so it's not dislocated; and go with the bandage according to the condition of the back some dressed wounds, and strengthen that with a strict dressing; and then consider the bandage at all times, and when it is softened, strengthen it.
And if necessity provokes a detachment of that which causes corrosion or rubbing, or pain, or abscess that occurs in the area, then touch and detach that and eradicate the plaster and rectify what occurs with the hand of skill of cure of it; and then reduce the plaster, if at times it adheres to the face; and adjust to it the strictness within which it will be healed.
Chapter 3.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are fractured.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in coarse locations that are near the back; and of their extremities, from the partition in front, nothing occurs to those contrary but because they are cartilaginous. The recognition of it is not hidden from the sensation that guides the examination of it with the fingers.
And the restoration should be such that it reduces the fracture with the fingers according to the possible method, until it is reduced as it should be, and then apply a plaster and strengthen the broken bone with steel, if necessary for that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declining into the partitions of the dent, then it happens to the patient vehement pain and puncture, like the puncture that happens to one who has pleurisy, and for this reason the bone puts on inflammation; and it happens difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of bloody mucus; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the medicines are prepared for that with many preparations.
And of it they say: it is good that you give the patient foods that cause inflammation and swelling, so that the belly is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed away from the affected parts.
We would have avoided this for what is not a trivial cause to generate a cold abscess, if it does not happen; and if it already happens, then it already helps with that.
And they said some of them that the ventosa should be given and also that it should suck with strength; and it seems reasonable, but however it is feared that the ventosa will attract the superfluities to the place due to weakness.
They will say some of these: that the place should be covered with wool that has already been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, which should be the binding plaster when you envelop the aforementioned retortion; and the patient should be treated with a cure of pleurisy of food and medicine.
And if the cause creates severe constriction for the patient who cannot breathe, and the bone presses on the vein with a sharp puncture, they should place a bandage over the patient, then they should make an incision on the place, uncovering the broken rib, and place their hand on that instrument which holds the diaphragm in place, the memory of which is preceded, and they should facilitate the bone, and extract that thing.
And then adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess forms, then cut it and pour powders in heated rose oil and apply to the place, and the patient should be treated with what is suitable for the internal abscess.
And let him rest on the side where he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter nine.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Some wounds imply fracture to the bones of the neck, and similarly injure them, and likewise to the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: for if the muscles are already softened and numbed and deadened and cannot be moved or stretched or strengthened, and when you constrict them with your fingers or prick them with a needle, you don't feel pain in them, then you should know that it is not curable, and in such cases it is often fatal.
And if you touch those areas and feel contraction in the fingers and prickling, then you should know that the nut of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see a similar thing in the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if it will be healed or not, then examine their feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, it happens to them as it does in the others, and when you put them on their back, there is loss of control and expulsion outside of their will, and some wounds make them be turned over on their back and want to urinate but cannot, then you should know that it is fatal, and therefore do not bother with their treatment.
Maybe if none of those things happen, the cause is better and more legible.
And the treatment of it is that you perform a cauterization on the abscess, placing it on the spine or between the shoulder blades, or in the armpits, for several times a day until the abscess is seated.
Then place one of the comforting and exciting plasters on it, and bind it with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where they feel pain until they are healed.
And if it happens that the bone slips or any cause that has already been separated from it, then cut on the tendon and show that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then heal that with some greasy ointment, until it is healed.
Maybe if the end of the bone is broken, then cut so that you insert the thumb of the left hand in the anus, and adjust the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second adjustment; and then place on it a plaster and bandage, if needed, and bind it.
If you feel shattered shards in it, then make a poultice on that area, and remove that, and cure the wound with the treatment that is preceded by until it is healed.
And if there are fractures in the head, then
Of the restoration of the width or On the restoration of the narrowness/stricture/con
The bones of the narrow parts are fragile and fractured, and if they fracture, then the break is not just a communication in the extremities of those but are cut in the length and decline to the separated parts; and it happens to the patient pain in the area and puncture, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over that until it is above the fracture, which is the shape/form of it.
And if the fracture is in the extremity of it only, then you bind it according to what appears to you as proper for binding until it is assembled into its natural shape.
Moreover, if the fracture is in the long [bone] or that which already declines to the separated parts, then you make the patient lie on their stomach so that it is prepared for you, the restoration of that fracture. And then when you have bound it, put a plaster on it, and place on that a splint of wood or of leather, and tighten with a bandage over which you do not fear puncture of fracture nor movement of the splint.
Engage the cavity with what fills it so that it takes the stricture according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on their side or over the side of the bed.
If the abscess bursts, then cease the extension of it and its restoration until the abscess is sedated, and place on it what will calm it according to what has been previously said; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it in this way as is suitable.
And if it happens in the hollow bones or they are comminuted, then cease the treatments.
Chapter 11.
On the restoration of the fracture of the helper/ assistant (or possibly "of the joint").
The helper/joint is what is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two manners, of which one is that you take a wooden splint according to this form:
And let there be two ligaments in the two extremities, and then let it be suspended from an elevated place.
And let the patient sit on a chair; and then put the broken arm outside on the splint so that it reaches to the end of it, in the middle place where the curvature of the splint is attached.
And then suspend the affected limb above the heavy cause, or let the minister extend it to the parts of the hips; and then the doctor should check the fracture with the adjacent hands together, until the fracture returns to its proper place.
And the other method is that the patient should be lifted above the nape of the neck and the hand of the doctor's assistant should be suspended with a bandage; and then command two ministers that one of them should hold what is above the fracture with their hands; and the other should hold the pus from the wound; and each of them should extend to their respective part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then pull with a bandage on the fracture and also on that one with another bandage, and each of the two ministers should extend to both their respective parts.
And thus if the fracture is near the extremity of the muscle, it is proper that one half of the bandage should be placed on the cilic and the other behind the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, it is proper that the bandage should be placed on that location and also above the elbow.
Wrap that one with ease and effort, and then as soon as it is wrapped as it is proper and applied with good application, then pull it tight if there isn't an abscessed cut.
More often if there is an aposteme abscess, then put a strict bandage around the place for eight days, and apply to it a woolen cloth soaked in warm oil and rosewater so that the abscess bursts, and then you will strengthen that area.
And the method of strengthening that area is to put a plaster on the fracture, and then apply a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm over that support, and place your hand opened over the shoulder muscle.
And put the cloth and strengthen it around the support of the arm so that the arm is in place of splints, if not prohibited by some cause of the broken bone.
Then use splints.
And put on the fracture that splint which is thickest and strongest of the other splints; and put between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break with an addition of three fingers from the whole part.
And then strengthen around the splints with the bandage that was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage on the place of the fracture should be tighter. And when the fracture is more elongated, the bandage should be less tight.
And then if you examine the positioning of the stars and the strictness as we have said in the hour in which you restore the member to its natural position, then you do.
If you suspect a post-stroke abscess, then loosen the tight dressing and insert the metal rods into the affected area as we have previously described.
And then consider the ligature in all parts for three days so that there is no accident in the place of swelling or inflammation; or it is forbidden for food to reach the member due to excessive constriction; and thus correct everything according to what we have said.
More so if you are confident that no accident will occur from this, then do not untie the ligature except after three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands be over their stomach.
And apply to the injured area a poultice full of warm wool, and consider every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken member is not deformed or dissolved, or that the ligature is not loosened, so correct all this according to your skill.
And feed the patient according to the order in which we have previously indicated, so that the food is subtle at the beginning until it is fully digested; then increase the quantity of their food.
It is customary for the injury and the bandage of it to be tightened for 40 days; then they should be untied and a bath administered, and treated with ointments that correct that condition.
If the fracture is properly aligned, then you should not remove the dressing and the splints for one or two days.
Chapter 12.
On the restoration of fractures of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two forearm bones; of which one is small, and it is the one that is called the radius, and the other is large and it is the one that is placed beside the small one, from the part below.
And it happens that only the larger forearm bone is broken, or the smaller one, and sometimes they are both broken together.
And when the smaller forearm bone is broken below, then the restoration of it is easier, and the healing of it is also easier.
And when the forearm bone on the left side is broken, the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken at the same time.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone below, then it is convenient for the doctor, in order to restore it, to make a small extension with ease so that it can be straightened.
But if the larger forearm bone is the one that is broken, then it is necessary to make a more vigorous extension.
And if both ankles are simultaneously sprained, then it is advisable that you apply a more forceful external traction.
And it is advisable that you place the figure of the hand in a way that restores and extends the patient's arm over a cushion, and be the pulse of the hand at the separated parts, more than all the other fingers, and the auricular finger be lower than the other fingers, and the patient be seated on a low, quilted chair.
And there should be a cushion in front of them at the height or level of their thigh, so that the patient does not have to strain themselves.
Then let the assistant extend the arm of the affected part with their hand, or with a bandage, and another assistant similarly extends the other.
And then the doctor should put the bone back in place so that it is reduced according to the best position that it can.
And if the bone's fracture is displaced, then you should make a perforation to reduce the entire mass in its proper place with your skill.
And if it appears that the displaced part is separated and the ligament is torn and it's not in your power to restore it, then you should put a splint on it and immobilize it according to the method that we have mentioned above.
More often if the fracture is on the thigh, you have already made a special chapter for it, and so take the cure of that.
And if an abscessed cautery happens at the beginning of your restoration, then anoint a cloth made with wax and white ceruse and oil of rose, and be moderate between thickness and subtlety, and apply the cloth strictly so that the abscess is eased; and then remove the wax and apply a prepared plaster made from powder of ground clay and the clearness of urine, and put on bandages; and let the bandage be the one that is applied on that closed and strengthened fracture.
And you should know that the name of the bandages of the arm is seen in the major preparations, or it is a fracture in one splint or in two splints together.
And apply your bandage strongly on the place of the fracture, firmly and vigorously, and when it is more constricted to the parts above the fracture or to the lower parts of it, loosen the bandage a little softer according as the memory of it is preceded in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, and not harsh nor rough, and the thread with which it is tied should be moderately of its own proper size between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
Examine the limb and its ligament closely for several days. And if a cause arises that requires correction, such as swelling of the limb, then it is necessary that you immerse the limb in hot water until the swelling subsides.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then apply the bandage tightly.
And if the fracture is already loosened and the bone is already realigned and resembles its original shape, then correct all that with your skill.
And consider more so if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess of constriction, then it is necessary that you loosen it a little and leave it for several days until it returns to its normal state, and then strengthen it.
More so, if to the patient no cause of those that we have mentioned happens, then it is not necessary that it be untied for about twenty days or around that.
And then let the hand of the patient be suspended by its own power of agitated movement, and make it sleep on its back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days, and all that depends on the disposition of the complications and the dispositions of the virtue.
Chapter thirteen.
On the restoration of fractures of the knee and fingers.
It is known that to the knee and joints of the fingers, fracture often occurs, and it does not happen to them unless rough and violent movements are applied.
And when fracture or injury occurs to the knee, then it is good that the patient keeps their leg straight and their heel aligned with their buttocks, and place their leg extended.
Then let the minister extend the broken bones and the doctor anoint those ends that are to be joined with good conjunction.
And then it is good to apply a plaster and stopper, unless an apostemous ulcer occurs; and place a dressing underneath according to the quantity of the location, and you have already wrapped that in linen cloth.
More so if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm of the hand, then make a figure of eight with cloth, and command the patient to hold that with their fractured palm; it should be held with a long bandage.
And let the dressing be wrapped in cloth in which there is padding, so that the dressing is applied to the palm; and hold it as is necessary.
If the fracture is in the separated parts of the forearm, then it is necessary to place a splint above and another below the separated part on the palm, so that the hand can be open and straight; and then put a bandage, as if the hand is wrapped with a ligature between the fingers.
And if a fracture occurs to one of the bones of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged according to what is necessary and put a splint on the palm if you want, place a small stick straight so that the fracture is elevated and not bent.
If the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle one, or the index, or the ring finger, or the little finger, then it should be bandaged and tied with a string around the finger itself; or all the fingers should be tied together continuously, which is better; or place on that finger a small stick, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your treatment, and then apply that, the disposition of a hot poultice and oppose  in your operation  to what is necessary when any of those causes occur, as it is reiterated in the narration of the case.
Chapter 14.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The bone of the hip joint is broken and declared by sensation, according to which it is converted into swelling in front and pus above.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that one bandage is strengthened over the fracture and another bandage placed below the fracture, and the patient lies on their stomach and on their face.
Then the minister extends all the bandage to its part according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle place of the bone.
More, if it is attached to the root of the hip, then let the bandage be attached to the groove at the part of the perineum, so that the extension comes in order, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly if the break is near the groin, then let the bandage be made to fit the groin, and let the extension be to the parts below.
And then the doctor should oil the bone with both his hands so that it reduces to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by good joining; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless the limb becomes gangrenous. More often if there are gatherings in the abscess, then you should wait for several days until the abscess is evacuated, and then return to your cure.
The stricture of it should be made thus: take a bandage that you wrap around the fracture, and fold it two or three times, and remove its excess; and then wrap the leg up to the ankle and to the root of the thigh.
And insert the end of the bandage between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the divisions of the joints, and let the ends of the bandage protrude from the division above the two parts, and then roll up onto the leg and the thigh what is excessive from the bandage.
And then put a splint on the thigh in the place of that fracture, and put one of them a splint on the bone of the leg.
And then fill the emptiness between the leg and the thigh with soft cloths, so that the stricture is consolidated.
And then start with a strong stricture in the middle on the place of the fracture with three or four wraps, and as you get used to it at the location of the fracture, your stricture should be lesser and more loose and softer.
And then extend the two extremities of the thigh to the divided parts of the hips until it reaches the hollow of the foot, and strengthen both extremities of the legs on the other side so that the ligament is not dislocated from its place.
And then put the structure on that, given that it does not cause corrosion or aposteme (infection) to the member, nor cautery (burns) or emflacio (swelling), and if any of those causes occur, then treat it with dissolution, and rectify what occurs, all properly, as we have said many times before.
More so, if there is a piece of bone in the bone that you can remove, then take it out as you are able; and if not, leave it alone and let it heal, and cure with what is previously mentioned, until the memory of it is healed.
And several times, this fracture of the thigh is strengthened by other things that are adjusted to its legs, as we have said in the assistant and in the arm.
But in the restoration of it, in such a way made, it does not cause the patient lameness; and if it is restored alone, another thing that must be applied to the other leg, then it is necessary that he limps because of this.
And you know that the thigh is strengthened in forty days or a few months or a few years, and all this depends on the diversity of the complications and other conditions.
Chapter 15.
On the restoration of the fracture of the collarbone of the shoulder.
Know that a fracture of the collarbone of the right shoulder is very painful, and more so if it reaches the nervous membranes beneath.
More so if it reaches the fracture, then it is not just a crack or hole in its divisions, and this can be with a wound or six wounds, and about this all has been stated according to sentiment.
And the restoration of it is such that what is broken from its divisions must be united with the words between them, so that it may be joined and conjoined as much as possible by equilibrium and ease and creation.
Then apply a plaster and put it on that round steel, if needed for that condition; and bind it with a bandage to hold it in place conveniently.
And then consider all the conditions that we have recommended for other fractures, just as it is with apostema and similar to those cases in which one must oppose all accidents to what is convenient for it, until it is healed.
Chapter sixteen.
On the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
You know that in the leg there are two bones, one of which is large and is named for the leg itself; and the other is delicate, and is named fibula.
And what happens to each of the species of fracture that happens to the two bones of the arm, for this reason restoration is made of it in the same way as restoration of the arm, and the operation is one.
And if both bones are fractured at the same time, the leg is turned into many fragments.
And if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is converted into fragments beforehand.
And if the larger bone is fractured, you will find that then, and it is one of those causes that cannot be amended.
Then administer extension and alignment and ligature of the bones according to that treatment.
But especially beware, if the fracture of the leg is an old and habitual one, that the extension should be less and more gentle, and alleviate your hand.
And in the leg there is some cause for operation on the arm, and it is when you straighten the bones and complete the entire operation, then you will take splints of wood from which are placed under the causes that were previously mentioned, and they should be made from branches of palm; choose from those the ones to which a small groove is petitioned, and not be of the knuckles, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the larger one.
And then wrap each bandage in two wraps according to the length of it, and place one wrap on the front part of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, and let the end of the bandage enter into the wound at the bottom location of the foot.
And then tie the two sticks in three places of both extremities and the middle place, for in this structure the leg is prevented from bending to the right and left sides, and it stays in good position.
And already a splint of wood has been administered according to the length of the leg; and let it be placed on it, the leg, so that it conserves that movement.
And moreover, take care that you do this in a fracture, and when one treats the wound properly.
And then consider each day the leg and examine it for previous study, and you guard it from apostema and inflammation or other similar causes; and if any of those causes happen, then apply to it what is necessary so that it may be healed.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the heel and of the bones of the foot and of the toes.
To the heel does not happen total fracture; but to the bones of the foot happens occasional fracture; and to the toes of the foot happens little fracture, and it doesn't happen to them except bruising in the middle of disposition.
And if the bones of the foot have a fracture and those bones need to be elevated above them, then the patient should place their foot on the ground prepared thus as before.
Then place one foot on what is elevated by those bones, and press those, and squeeze, and bend those until they turn in the places.
Then apply a plaster and stopper above, and place on the belly of the foot a small table to which are two clamps or clips.
And then stretch that on the foot with downward tension, since you have wrapped that on the foot with bandages and other things for which indications are given.
And when three or four days have passed, then undo the binding, for you will find the bones bent, or broken, or displaced, and the operation on them is thus as you would conjoin.
But if some of the said bones are broken, then restore them, and bend them thus as you know and are advised in the restoration of the bones of the hand. And then place on the broken finger a splint according to the length of the finger, and let it be bound with a little bandage; and place on the foot this table that is recommended to you, and stretch it with downward tension.
More often if it is broken into two or three pieces, then you will place a quantity of it wrapped in lion's cloth on each digit of the foot, and hold the table at the lowest part of the foot, and stand on one of the legs of the table to make the belly of the foot retain it well.
And it is suitable for you that you do not forget to consider all the causes which I have told you about in other fractures of accidents that we have mentioned; and apply this to every accident that suits it.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the knee of the thigh and of the bone of the kneecap and of the rod of the man.
When the knee of the thigh is broken, then make that plaster cast in a seated position, and incline it slightly towards the back of the two, a little, and let it be held from behind.
And let the bandage cover the knee of the thigh, and be made on the knee of that person like a spear; and let the thigh be bent, and raise the two small ones little by little, until all that is forgotten so that it is seen that the knee is thus as before and the broken bone returns.
And then pause to put powders on their back, and when they want to urinate, hold the container with ease and urine, and return it and take back that second according to the manner in which one mixes the medicine at the beginning, and then return to the first powder.
And make them do this for seven days or surround those with, because that is restored.
And if you want, take a piece of wool and thread a hemp thread through the trachea of it a tube; and insert the whole bladder into the fundament, and blow into the tube with virtue so that the bladder is inflated within the fundament, because the fracture returns.
And then it should be filled with liquid and stopped for some days according to what we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the penis of man or woman is fractured, then administer in the restoration and reduction of it that liquid which we have recommended for the hip bone.
And that which is written is not hidden in these nearby fractures and which few understand, mainly to whom some study and use and intend my free speech; because that fracture means for you in the major preparations of the way of writing in the restoration and in the strictness of it, and therefore understand well.
More in the wound of the man when it is pierced, take a poultice of water, and put the wound in it; and return your hand over the wrapping of cloth, and let it be wrapped, and return it be left alone for three days or around them, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
On the restoration of fracture of the bones when it is caused by a wound.
It is convenient for the one to whom a fracture due to a wound happens, and especially if the bone is large, such as the bone of the thigh or of the arm, or similar to that, which you consider and make incisions in the same manner as the conditions of surgery require, as we have said before.
And if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary that you make an incision for retention of it in such a way that you dust over it with powdered dragant, if another cause is not present.
Then begin to restore the fracture on the same day, and do not delay it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day at all, because you will cause bad accidents.
If the broken bone protrudes from the wound, then it is necessary that you reduce and set it with your hands for ease and minimal extension.
If extension is not according to your preparation and the patient's condition, then you reduce it with your hands using a device; this device is made of iron, its length is seven or eight fingers and its width depends on the size of the wound; and for this reason, the doctor needs to take three or four of them depending on the quantity indicated by the treatment of each type of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it be in minimal thickness so that it is not doubled over the compression during the operation.
And let it be with sharp extremities where the edges are smooth; and let its thickness be tending to coarse, and of its width extending into the partitions.
And this is its shape:
It is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", which means little spade or small chisel.
So that the extremity of the sharp one is elevated above the extremity of the bone that protrudes.
Insert a probe into the opening so that the bone returns, and let it be treated with some ointment.
And therefore make an effort to treat the extremities of the fracture between them, and if the fractured extremity is subtle, and does not take the ointment well, then it is necessary that you insert the extremity of that bone so that the ointment has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone to what we have recommended, then insert it with what is convenient for him of the insertions that we have said; or saw it with one of the saws in whichever manner is possible for you.
And then scratch what remains in the bone of roughness and of sparse scratches.
And when the bone is reduced, and with the reduction the patient finds vehement pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and therefore do what you conferred with the patient previously.
When the restoration that you do to the bone is completed, then submerge a cloth in white vinegar, and specifically if it is in summer, and do not put anything on the wound such as wax or another cause in which there is oil, so that it does not induce pus and corruption.
And then administer steel in the hour of the complement of the restoration of the bone, and leave the wound uncovered so that scar tissue forms from the outer layers and uses bandages according to the quantity of the wound.
And beware with all caution that you do not touch the wound to the fracture, because many times it has been seen that foolish doctors cause death or corrosive illness, except for being sane. And if the bone's structure loosens, becomes soft, different from the structure of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or is a bad wound, and you suspect those accidents worse than which we have mentioned, and you find pain in the area, then do not put steel on it, but rather make a dressing of durable cloths in place of steel and apply it with those.
And when for one or two days the wound begins to suppurate, and pus has formed in it, then remove the cloth that you had put on it with vinegar, and administer warm poultices, using those of our custom, which is to treat wounds, such as tetrafarmacon ointment and similar things.
And it is suitable for you to dress the limb and consider the wound every morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed, and it is good that the member be prepared in such a way that pus can run from it to the lower parts with ease.
If the wound does not heal after many days, and it is neither consolidated nor separated from the arm, then you should know that this is a sign of small ulcers that will appear on the skin because the wound has become infected. And of those ulcers, those that are separated will remain and be extracted, those that do not separate from the flesh will cause the member to swell and create pain, you must make an incision in them according to what is possible for you.
And if there appears to the wound pus or ichor, it is a sign of corruption and putrefaction, then you should apply one of those remedies that is suitable for it from the treatment of which the aforementioned narration is preceded in the following chapter.
And in this case, you should place your sermon and present your wisdom: when the bone is fractured, apply it to the member like the bone of the thigh and of the hip and similar to those of the large members, and do not suppose that there will be an attraction of it, nor its extraction, because many times death occurs due to this; having removed the thing that presses, hold firmly for twenty days or thirty; then treat the wound if it is visible to you, and if not, let it be.
Chapter twenty.
On the treatment of swelling that occurs in the vicinity of some fracture.
Excessive movement causes this swelling in the vicinity of a healing fracture, and especially when it is near the joints, and it is worsened by the shape of the limb; and therefore it is prohibited to let the limb perform its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer those medicines that have a contracting effect, such as aloes, enses, mira, sercacollo, acrassia, and similar ones; take some or all of these, and mix them with six-year-old wine, or with white alum, or with sour vinegar, and apply this to the swelling in a poultice, and constrict it firmly with a good bandage.
And leave the poultice on for several days without removing it; and then remove it and apply another one, until the swelling has subsided; or spread a layer of lead plaster on it, because lead has the property of reducing all inflammation in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned into a stone and is hardened, then it's necessary to provoke an abscess, and then cut it open through the upper part of the limb, and remove the excess and prominent parts, or treat it with some corrosive remedies until it subsides; and cure it until it heals.
Chapter 21.
Of restoration of fracture when it is restored and then the thin member and the bruise are reduced according to its own nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and then the thin member and bruise are reduced, this is not done except for several reasons: among which one is the excessive solution of the ligament and the loosening of what was previously bound; or due to an excess of tightness of the ligaments through which nourishment is prohibited from flowing to the member; or due to an excess of superfluous swelling; or a movement that is not at its proper time; or lack of blood in the body of the patient and weakness of it.
And the cure of this is to give food to the patient and to make their heart tender so that blood may be multiplied in them; and administer a bath; and apply warm and soothing treatments on them.
And then apply a plaster on the member, so that the plaster may draw to it much nourishment, or continue a swelling of warm water, until nourishment flows and returns to its natural figure.
Chapter 22.
Of the cure of broken bones when they are restored crookedly and the operation of them is prohibited from what is suitable.
When the member is restored to its proper place after injury or fracture, and swelling and nodulation occur at the broken and bent bone, it is because of this that the shape of the member becomes deformed, but even though the member does not prohibit its natural operation, however it is not suitable that you should heed the words of one who claims that the member will never function normally again after a new fracture.
Many foolish doctors and restorers have done this in our land; and this operation is very shameful, inducing great fear, the signal of which is mortal.
If the stiffness and nodulation are recent, then it is fitting that they be wrapped in warm water in which have been cooked softening herbs, such as leaves of altea and roots of it, and mallow, and similar things; and let a plaster of mollifying plaster be made for it, such as diacalabon well made; or that the mucilage of the root of altea be taken and mixed with gum of walnut or with oil of cypress, and applied to it; or that fat figs be crushed with females of colomb, and similar things of the medicines which are called by name consolidating.
And sometimes it is dissolved by continuous friction with a light touch on the most humid areas, and movement of the member should be administered in all parts at all times.
When the tortuosity has already advanced and is made strong and converted into stone and necessity calls for treatment with iron, then it is necessary that the worst of the abscess be cut out and the continuation of the bone be dissolved, and let what is superfluous in the node of that member be cut away with subtle incisions, and facility be administered in that solicited and studied area.
Chapter 23.
On speech about dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of some joint from its proper place, by which movement is hindered, and the shape of the member is distorted, and brings to the patient pains and great lesions.
Therefore when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to perforate as soon as possible so that it can be reduced, and do not delay the reduction, because if it is delayed, the area becomes infected and strong with what is made, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason it is not advisable to touch or stretch during the time of infection, because many times it brings about spasms and painful sores.
When this happens, then it is necessary that you apply the flannel to the patient's body, and let it be left on until the infection subsides; and let the member be wrapped in warm water and oil, and then let it be reduced with ease.
Every member should be treated with that which memory will bring back to its place.
And you have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture; from the upper part of the body to the lower part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
When both jaws are dislocated and do not articulate; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are returned to their places with little movement, and slightly loosened; or they are completely dislocated and reduced, until they are loosened at the joint, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot hold it nor close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped in his words.
More often when the dislocation of it is slight, then in major conditions it returns by itself for a slight cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and complicated, then it is necessary that reduction of it be administered promptly, and not delayed at all.
And it is that the minister should hold the head of the patient, and the doctor insert his fingers of one hand in the root of the dislocation of the jaw inside the mouth of the patient, if the dislocation is on one side; or insert both hands together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of their hands be outside with which they hold; and command the patient to cease moving his jaws and loosen them from each part; and the doctor should take hold of the jaw and pull it until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction of it is difficult, especially when both jaws are dislocated, then administer evaporation with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is done easily and the reduction of both is not delayed, thus as we have said.
And then when they are returned and still swollen, and the patient's mouth is closed and not loosened, then place on those swellings a poultice of wax and cerate, and make it with wax and mixed oil; and apply it with ease and soft bindings.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be held between two pillows, so that they do not turn to the right or left; and do not force them to chew anything; having their food nearby, forbid them from eating lion, until when the pains are lessened and the jaws are gnashed, then let them eat what they want.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth until after eating and drinking and digestion, until the jaw is gnashed and healed.
And if the reduction of two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any hour and not converted to their proper place, then give bitter medicines and constant soda, and if the patient's stomach is upset, and they vomit and have pure bile.
And when you see that, then know that it is a loss; and many times death occurs to the one to whom this condition happens in X days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the shoulder and of the extremity of the muscle.
The shoulder is not dislocated from the part that is joined to the chest; and sometimes it is dislocated at the outer part, and this is declared by sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back with two pillows and extends their arms. And then bind the area with your palm with strong binding, so that it will return to its place.
And then put a plaster on that place and dust it and sprinkle it.
But the extremity of that which is joined to the body and continues with it, is not dislocated on any day; then it is necessary that it be reduced and put in place according to what we have said and according to what is appropriate.
And then put a plaster on that place, and dust, and extract, and command the patient to adhere to quietness and to rest until they are healed.
And with this medical treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter twenty-six.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
You should know that the muscle is not dislocated in one way, but rather in three manners, of which one is dislocation at the top of the tendon to the top of the joints.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the bottom of the muscle, and this is done with tension; and it is not dislocated at the back parts for the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front parts because of the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and pushed out at the bottom parts of the tendon, and mostly in those where the flesh is weak, it is easily dislocated; but in those where there are many muscles, it is pushed out with difficulty.
And for this reason some men suffer from percussion or blow, and the muscle of it is affected by a hot cauterization, and it is treated with that which was already dislocated.
Therefore, I advise you to apply this within the limits of your knowledge, and then proceed with caution in the treatment of it.
And the dislocation of it is recognized when it is at the bottom parts of the tendon; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy one, because you find among them manifest diversity; and you find in the head of the muscle a hollow and at the bottom of the tendon of the head of the muscle like a tube, and the patient cannot move their hand to their ear, nor can they move it for any movement.
If the foot or ankle is dislocated, then you will obviously notice a visible deformation, and it is not hidden.
And from this dislocation, a lighter reduction is made when it is fresh and the patient is an infant.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises the hand of it to the divided parts; and then places the tied pouices on the thigh of it, and raises the joint with strength in its own place, and the minister raises the hand of it, and extends it in its; and then holds it until it returns lightly.
But if because of what we have said it does not return, and thus the dislocation persists for many days, then it is advisable that the patient be bathed in warm water and administered a softening enema that moistens and loosens, as if one were to cook turnip, and fenugreek, and melillot in water, and have it administered.
And then the patient should be placed on their back and held in place by the thigh of a servant in wait of a ladder that is between looseness and strictness, and then the doctor places his heel on the sphere, and raises the head of the muscle with virtue, and pulls the hand of the patient so it is not mixed with the divided parts, and another servant holds the head of the patient, so it is not mixed with the divided parts, until it returns to its place.
And if you wish, return that second segment in this manner: make sure a man is present who is taller than the patient, and let him be on the side of that rib, and insert his muscle under the patient's buttocks and lift the patient's buttocks to the divisions above so that the patient is suspended in air, and another minister pulls the patient's hand from the divisions below the patient's belly. But if the patient is lightweight, it is good that someone else holds them, so that another cause is suspended, for which is heavy, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is returned immediately in another manner: make sure a long stick is placed on the ground, the end of which is rounded in shape, like a pillow, not too big or too small, and place it under the patient; then place them on top of the stick's head, so that the patient stands according to the length of the stick, and extend their hand to the divisions below the other part; and let their body be extended on the other side with strength, so that the joint returns to its place with speed.
And if the reduction of it is difficult by all the causes that we have mentioned, then use this cure: make sure you take a stick whose length is two arms and width is four fingers, and its thickness is adjusted to the size of the thigh according to this shape:
And then place on the head a dressing of warm cloths so that the patient does not get a chill.
And then let the patient's bed be raised, and let the hand or arm be extended over the chest between the ribs, and let the body be wrapped around the hips and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
And then let the arm be placed on a ladder according to its width, and the hand be extended between the ribs, and the other limb be suspended from the other side, so that the joint is in a balanced position.
And when the treatment of it is completed according to each manner that is possible for you, then take a sufficient amount of flax seed and wool of equal quantity; and then apply a plaster prepared from powder of mulberry and white of egg on all the muscle, in such a way that it is wrapped around, and then bind the bed tightly with a decent strap; and the ligament be wrapped around the plaster, and the hand of it be tied to the neck of it, and be left so, and not be moved for seven days.
And it is good if the food of the patient is sparse, until the member is comforted, for it is easier for their healing.
And then the food should be given in large quantities and a exercise of movement should be practiced; and so if it is stopped and not softened, it is already healed.
But if the joint is dislocated with excessive moisture that occurs to it or for another cause, then it is advisable that a cautery with three punctures be administered there, as it is said in the chapter on cauterization.
And if it is done and after seven days the ligament and the joint are not strengthened, and the plaster and the bandage are removed from it with some moisture, and it is not strengthened and can't hold and can't raise that arm to the divisions above, then know that the nerves of it, which are at the head of the muscle, are already injured or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
Of the dislocation of that which is done to the foot and the thigh or the back parts, the reduction is made with a push and extension with the hands until it returns.
And let another bandage be administered to it, and cure it until it is healed.
And if there occurs to it the heaviness of healing in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use warm baths with moisture until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its first nature.
Chapter 27
On the treatment of dislocation of the hip.
You should know that the joint of the hip is dislocated with difficulty and in the same way returns with difficulty; and this one is dislocated in all parts, and mainly in the front part before the back part, and the dislocation of it is not hidden from you when you put your hands on the victim or touch according to whatever figure is dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated hip to its place, it is declared manifestly to you, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor touch that muscle with the shoulder.
And it is necessary to treat the hip, and reduce the dislocation as much as possible before it comes to a critical point, because if it reaches the critical point, the reduction of it is difficult, and many times it does not heal completely; and mainly when it affects the back parts, because this one is worse than all other types of dislocations, and of major pain, and many times death occurs from it.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce, is that the hand of the patient should be extended to both sides, and their arm should be spread out, and the hands of the doctor should be on the hip and on the back of the patient, and they press the joint with the fingers of their hands together, or with the heel of their palm until it returns to its place.
More often if the dislocation is in the front parts, then return it thus that you double your hand with their hand, while striking the heel with the bottom of their palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey the reduction, then administer a vehement extension and strong movement.
And it is that two assistants stretch the arm, and hold that joint with two assistants so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then wrap the arm from one end to the other, since it is enveloped in double-wound long cloth over their hand, or bandage.
And when the doctor touches the joint with their hand, anoint that with some oil so that it has those causes which help in lubricity of the joint with ease; and then smear the joint with strong ointment while it returns.
And then the reduction of it being placed on that plaster in which is stipulated and exception with album of egg, and be restrained with strict bandage, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and be left for some days.
And then it will be dissolved; and thus if the joint is formed in its own place, then you undo the bandage of it and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not strengthened, then apply the plaster and bandage, and leave that on for several days until it is reinforced; and then remove it.
But if it happens that the gravity affects him, then with the reduction of the joint and its being in movement, apply humidification in bath and subtle friction, and compression, until he is relieved; or place a poultice on the affected joint, which is called a warm poultice; and then tie it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And then remove that, and put him in a bath; and when he sweats, then rub the joint with tempered friction, and apply on it an ointment, which is called a Ronhon, one time, and the second, and the third. in a warm bath, until he is cleaned.
And if you want, place on that wound of the cow a humid and scratched poultice with butter and strong over it; and do this several times, because it is humid and reduces to its condition.
Chapter 28.
Of the cure of dislocation of the shoulder, which is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
In the case of a dislocated shoulder, the reduction of the dislocation is easier, and on the contrary, it is more difficult for other joints, but it is essential to have speed in the reduction of it at the time when it is dislocated, provided that the area is not infected or that an abscess forms.
And the reduction of the dislocation is that you place the joint of the patient's hand on a table and the assistant extends their arm, and the doctor places their hand or palm on the prominence of the joint, and pushes it in until it returns to its place.
But it is also necessary to consider if the palm has been dislocated towards the back of the hand, then the patient should place the back of their hand on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is in front of the hand, then the position of that hand should be intrinsically on the table, so that the doctor's hand can act on the prominence of the joint.
And so if it returns to its place quickly, that's good; and if not, then force that arm with a plaster cast, putting pressure on the abscess and relieving it; and do not repeat this, because if not, there will be no relief nor power over the reduction of it, since the days pass.
We prepare the joint according to its curvature; and we do not apply any cause to the patient's harm, but rather the hand is softened and cannot hold any cause; then we know that the nerves are cut or torn, and then it is not a problem for them except that it should be strengthened with a cautery near the wound and reinforce their confidence by applying some cause.
Thus when the sickness is returned, then let it rest on the plaster that we have recommended, and let it be loose and strengthened for several days; and then let it be unbound and let it rest and the hand of the patient should be exercised.
We see that the movement of it is stopped and if any heaviness occurs in it, then we apply warm water and friction with some clothing, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of displacement of the fingers.
Some bandages are used to dislocate the fingers entirely.
And when a finger is displaced among those at the palm or the belly of the hand, then hold the finger and press the displacement with the thumbs, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that one from the part to which it is displaced, and let it rest for two days.
And then lift that one and stretch the one that should be straightened in that diseased joint.
And when night comes, lay him in that position and do not cease to loosen it in the day and exercise with movement, and lay him at night; and do this for several days until it is straightened.
And do similarly to this, if it is dislocated at the belly of the hand, and lay it in that same place and do the same thing as the first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do the same to this when it is dislocated in the other parts.
Chapter thirty.
On the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When there occurs to some of the vertebrae of the back or neck a complicated dislocation, and many vertebrae are displaced from their place, then in that case there is no cure, for death follows the patient.
And the sign of this is that the evacuation of the patient is outside of their will, and they cannot retain it; and many members of theirs are weakened, such as some limbs or two legs or two arms, or one of those; or a vertebra is displaced from its place, for many are displaced and many are made to lose control of those in four parts.
Therefore, that one is already improved in the parts behind and is called recovery or health.
More, the cure of it is that then you consider if the recovery happens due to purification, because then in that there is no cure nor is total healing of it.
But in that which comes from accident or percussion or similar to these, the Ancients have already said the manners of cure with prolix speech, from which the most returns to profit; but I have already abbreviated it for the sake of avoiding too much of it, due to its proximity to my intention, to which I have explained and formulated from diverse instruments, with which I will show and expound.
And of those, none are cured except the one that happens on the back properly with what I have recommended.
And it is that the patient should be stretched out on their face on a low bench near a wall, and should be stretched with a soft cloth so that the chest of theirs is not constricted.
And then place a wooden stick on the ground at the end of the bed around the patient's head, and another stick in the air under their feet at the other end of the bed.
One minister holds the stick, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another minister holds the other according to that method.
And then wrap around the patient's head and tie the first bandage tightly; and stretch the end of the bandage to the stick which is near the patient's head, and tie it on them; and then tie the other bandage around their hips and their thighs; and adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other stick which is near their feet.
And then each minister stretches the bandage to the part of the stick, and let the two sticks and the bandages in their respective places where they are fixed, but however not tied as we have said.
The doctor places his palms on the spine with strength until it returns to its place, or places on that one table, and then adjusts on the table the patient's feet until they return to their place.
But if it does not return to its place by this method, then take a table whose width or length is in the air about three arms, and hollow out in the part of which we have said is near the patient a place where the end of the table enters. And then put a hand on the patient's back, the doctor puts their feet on the other extremity, and tightens so that the spine is constricted and returns to its place.
And if you want, take the turn which is wound on the hand; and it is that you make on the ground two posts of the patient's feet, with a length of an arm, and two posts at the back of their feet also of the same length; and let there be a space between each post of one palm, and already made in each post a hole through which the turn passes, and each post is firmly fixed to the ground so that it does not move.
And there be a wooden post in which the turn is made, and wound through the two holes of the two posts.
And on the extremity of it there is a hole in which the post is formed, the length of which is a palm where it is wound; and on the two other posts is something similar to that.
And then tighten the ligaments that you have tied on the chest of the patient in the hole which is at the head of it, and the ligaments that you have tied on its thighs in the turn which is at their feet.
And then each turn must have a minister who will turn the turn with their hand, and the doctor will anoint the spine according to what we have said.
And then when the spine is returned to its place, and the area is anointed, then take a plaster made with egg whites, and apply it; and put it on the plaster of the table from which the width should be three fingers or around that, and the length should be the quantity that takes the space of the swelling, and on the part of the spine apply it.
And then let it be tied with a bandage as we have said, and use on the patient a subtle nourishment so that they may be healed.
And if there remains some weakness in the area at the end of the healing, then take medicines that soften and lubricate for administration on the table for a long time, which we have recommended; and already administered in that place is a plaster of lead.
And some times it happens that there is an emergency in the back of the spine, and it is thought that it is due to dislocation; and it is that bone for which we have already warned you, so that no absorption should be made with this medicine, because if you do, it will bring about your death.
Chapter twenty-one.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
It is known that the joint of the hip and the junction of the muscle do not just cause dislocation, but rather only the displacement, and does not happen to those what happens to other joints with little movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways, that is, it is dislocated to the inner parts, or to the outer parts, or to the front parts, or to the rear parts.
And mostly it is dislocated to the inner parts, and rarely to the front parts or to the rear parts.
And the sign of dislocation to the inner parts is that, when you adjust the leg of the healthy side to the sick one, it is longer and the knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the sick leg cannot bend upwards without lifting the hip, and this is the place where the hip bone is separated from its joint, because the head of the thigh bone has already come out of its socket.
And the sign of that one to whom dislocation happens to the outer parts is that its symptoms are contrary to this dislocation.
If there is dislocation in the patient's jaw due to an accident, then it expands their own leg according to the injury, but especially does not double the pain that is in the tooth, and if it perforates to the nostril and cannot be retained at the front teeth; and the urine of theirs is retained and their throat is abscessed, and you should ambulate them on the ankle.
If there is dislocation in the patient's jaw at the back teeth, then it does not expand the tooth, nor can it double the front tooth that doubles the throat, and their leg is shorter than the other, and the throat is softened, and the head of the thigh is turned to that place.
Moreover, the reduction of the species of this dislocation is what you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, that has already been antiquated with them having that applied treatment, and you administer reduction according to their disposition, then it is not entirely in that cure; and therefore it does not matter that a prognosis be made in that case.
But in that case where the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four species of dislocation, then you should reduce it to what will return the joint and extend it to the inner and outer parts, and move it to the right and left, because it strongly returns and does not indicate another cure.
Mays, if it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong minister and extend the leg of the patient from the affected part, or with their hands, or with a bandage which is tied over the knee; and another minister extends the hand of that one in such a way that they place their hands under the testicles of the patient; and place a strong bandage over the joint of the thigh, and hold another minister to hold the extremity of the bandage.
And if it is an extension of the patient or of the affected part to the ankle joint, or from the back part to the buttocks area.
And if this extension is of all those things in a way that the patient is elevated with their body off the ground and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is a common species to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns to what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary to apply a specific treatment that I will speak of regarding its own cure.
In the reduction of its own, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is in the inner parts, it is necessary that the patient be placed on their back; and place bandages over the joint of the thigh in that location which is between the head of the thigh and the place where the ankle is; and then extend the bandage from the part of the ankle to the furthest part of the buttocks.
And then another strong minister takes the patient's arms, and presses the thick part of the thigh where the pain is, extending it to the affected parts with vigorous extension, so that it returns to its proper place.
And this species is of minor cure on which the member is reduced more than the other species.
But if you are excused from this and do not obey the entrance to this species of cure at all, then it is good that both feet of the patient be bound together with a strong, light bandage, above both the ankles and both the toes.
And let the length of each one be four fingers, and let the patient's leg be extended more than the other by two fingers.
And then the patient is suspended over the head of the stool, and it is in the mud; and let the length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong youth to bend the head of the thigh, and let another youth be suspended with the patient, and the other youth bending the thigh with strength, so that the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in proper reduction, when there is dislocation of the affected part, it is good that the patient put a poultice as we have recommended in the aforementioned gelbozitat; and let the bandage be tightened over the patient's leg properly and over their chest.
And then place two pads, one on the patient's feet and the other on their head; and then place adjusted pads on the middle of the poultice applied with humid cloths wrapped around it, so that the patient is not damaged by the pad being between their thighs, for which you must extend the edges to the divided parts so that they are not attracted.
And then let each assistant extend to their own part, and the doctor with his hand guide the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, it is better that the leg be extended gently, and it is according to this narrative about poultices; and the doctor places the palm of their right hand on the patient's shoulder, and then cuts with the other hand, and with this cutting places the extended part to the front part at the front of the jaw.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, it is not good that the patient's legs be extended, and it is elevated from the ground; rather it is better that it be placed on a hard surface, just as it is better if their hip is dislocated to the front parts, according to what we have said about placing them on two hips; and it is placed on its face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, there is no error.
And it is advisable that compression be administered on the table over the area where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should be  and the sign of the reduction is not hidden from you, and that is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those limbs and the patient stretches their own leg and expands those six excuses, then you know that the member as it should be is returned  and then you adjust the two hips and apply a plaster cast and stretch with a bandage so that the width is not hindered in any of the divisions; and have the patient rest for three days or four.
Then remove the bandage and the plaster cast, and compare one hip to the other.
Then, if you see those limbs in alignment, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed, and allow the patient to walk.
And if you see any cause of softening, then return and plaster that, and stretch it according to your first instruction, and let it be for three days.
Then remove that, and have them delay walking on it for some days, until it is comforted.
Chapter twelve.
On the cure of displacement of the tooth.
The tooth is displaced in three ways, for it is displaced to the front parts, and to the rear parts, and to the upper parts more than to the lower parts; and to the adjacent parts it is not displaced.
And the sign of displacement of it is that you command the patient to apply their leg to the thigh; then you will know that the tooth is displaced.
And the restoration of all its kinds is that you make the patient stand straight, and also that they extend their leg if possible; place their two hands on the face, and hold their foot between the two hands; and then make them press the tooth with their palms, and hold those between the teeth strongly over the tooth, and another assistant makes them extend their foot until the tooth returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction of it is that the leg sticks to the thigh with lightness without effort.
And then apply a plaster and make the leg stick to the thigh, and then tie those together with a bandage for three days or four, and then remove it.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until they are healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it to you is excused, then administer a strong extension with bandages in the cure of the hip as previously mentioned narration.
Chapter thirty-three.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle sometimes is injured by slight movement, and sometimes it is dislocated according to the injury, and the dislocation of it is made at the inner or outer parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that the ankle is swollen, proceeding from the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of the sprain of it is of gentle reduction, and it is that it should be extended with ease, with bandages, and kept still until it returns.
But in the treatment of it when it is dislocated according to the injury, it is necessary that you make the patient stand upright, and have a strong and able assistant at the back of the patient in the middle location of it.
